                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-09-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Human utilization of the mulberry&#8211;silkworm interaction started at least 5,000 years ago and greatly influenced world history through the Silk Road. Complementing the silkworm genome sequence, here we describe the genome of a mulberry species Morus notabilis. In the 330-Mb genome assembly, we identify 128&#8201;Mb of repetitive sequences and 29,338 genes, 60.8% of which are supported by transcriptome sequencing. Mulberry gene sequences appear to evolve ~3 times faster than other Rosales, perhaps facilitating the species&#8217; spread worldwide. The mulberry tree is among a few eudicots but several Rosales that have not preserved genome duplications in more than 100 million years; however, a neopolyploid series found in the mulberry tree and several others suggest that new duplications may confer benefits. Five predicted mulberry miRNAs are found in the haemolymph and silk glands of the silkworm, suggesting interactions at molecular levels in the plant&#8211;herbivore relationship. The identification and analyses of mulberry genes involved in diversifying selection, resistance and protease inhibitor expressed in the laticifers will accelerate the improvement of mulberry plants. Mulberry trees are the primary food source for silkworms, which are reared for the production of silk. In this study, He et al. present the draft genome sequence of Morus notabilisand find that it evolved significantly faster than other plants in the Rosales order.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Mulberry trees are the primary food source for silkworms, which are reared for the production of silk. In this study, He et al. present the draft genome sequence of Morus notabilisand find that it evolved significantly faster than other plants in the Rosales order."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Human utilization of the mulberryâsilkworm interaction started at least 5,000 years ago and greatly influenced world history through the Silk Road. Complementing the silkworm genome sequence, here we describe the genome of a mulberry species <i>Morus notabilis</i>. In the 330-Mb genome assembly, we identify 128âMb of repetitive sequences and 29,338 genes, 60.8% of which are supported by transcriptome sequencing. Mulberry gene sequences appear to evolve ~3 times faster than other Rosales, perhaps facilitating the speciesâ spread worldwide. The mulberry tree is among a few eudicots but several Rosales that have not preserved genome duplications in more than 100 million years; however, a neopolyploid series found in the mulberry tree and several others suggest that new duplications may confer benefits. Five predicted mulberry miRNAs are found in the haemolymph and silk glands of the silkworm, suggesting interactions at molecular levels in the plantâherbivore relationship. The identification and analyses of mulberry genes involved in diversifying selection, resistance and protease inhibitor expressed in the laticifers will accelerate the improvement of mulberry plants.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Mulberry is a deciduous tree and is an economically important food crop for the domesticated silkworm, <i>Bombyx mori</i>. The practice of producing valuable silk from silkworms nourished by mulberry leaves started at least 5,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Barber, E. J. W. Prehistoric Textiles: The Development Of Cloth In The Neolithic And Bronze Ages With Special Reference To The Aegean Princeton University Press (1991)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1124">1</a></sup> and helped to shape world history through the Silk Road.</p><p>The family Moraceae comprises 37 genera with ~1,100 species, including well-known plants such as mulberry, breadfruit, fig, banyan and upas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Clement, W. L. &amp; Weiblen, G. D. Morphological evolution in the mulberry family (Moraceae). Syst. Bot. 34, 530â552 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1131">2</a></sup>. Mulberry belongs to the genus <i>Morus</i> with 10â13 recognized species and over a 1,000 cultivated varieties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Nepal, M. P. &amp; Ferguson, C. J. Phylogenetics of Morus (Moraceae) inferred from ITS and trnL-trnF sequence data. Syst. Bot. 37, 442â450 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1138">3</a></sup>, which are widely planted in the Eurasian continent, Africa and the United States. Mulberry leaf production for silkworm uses ~626,000 and 280,000 hectares of land in China and India, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="SÃ¡nchez, M. D. World distribution and utilization of mulberry, potential for animal feeding. FAO Electron. Conf. Mulberry Animal Prod. (Morus1-L) 1â11 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1142">4</a></sup>. Mulberry also attracts farmers for its delicious fruit, bark for paper production and multiple usages in traditional oriental medicine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kim, S. Y. et al. Antioxidative flavonoids from the leaves of Morus alba. Arch. Pharm. Res. 22, 81â85 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1146">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Asano, N., Tomioka, E., Kizu, H. &amp; Matsui, K. Sugars with nitrogen in the ring isolated from the leaves of Morus bombycis. Carbohyd. Res. 253, 235â245 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1149">6</a></sup>.</p><p><i>B. mori</i>, a lepidopteran model system and a specialist, feeds on mulberry leaves. The majority of known Lepidoptera species are herbivorous and are, therefore, economically important as major pests of agriculture and forestry. The adoption of silkworm rearing has led to intensive studies on feeding stimulants that are critical to the understanding of plantâinsect interactions. The genome sequencing of silkworm was completed in 2008 (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Xia, Q. et al. A draft sequence for the genome of the domesticated silkworm (Bombyx mori). Science 306, 1937 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1157">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Consortium, I. S. G. The genome of a lepidopteran model insect, the silkworm Bombyx mori. Insect Biochem. Mol. Biol. 38, 1036â1045 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1160">8</a>). However, very little genomic information is available for species in the genus <i>Morus</i>. Although the genomic sequence of mulberry will facilitate the improvement of mulberry plants, the mulberryâsilkworm genome pair will deepen our understanding of the fundamentals in plantâherbivore adaptation.</p><p>Here we report the draft genome sequence of a mulberry species (<i>M. notabilis</i>). The estimated 357-Mb genome of <i>M. notabilis</i>, composed of 7 chromosome pairs, is sequenced using Illumina technology to a 236-fold depth coverage. On the basis of the 330-Mb assembly genome, we identify 128âMb repetitive sequences and 29,338 protein-coding genes. Comparative genomic analyses reveal that mulberry evolved more rapidly than other sequenced Rosales. The identification and analyses of mulberry genes involved in resistance will accelerate the improvement of mulberry plants. The presence of predicted mulberry micro RNAs (miRNAs) in two tissues of the silkworm suggest probable interactions at molecular levels between the plantâherbivore pair.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We applied a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy to the mulberry species <i>M. notabilis</i>, which contains seven distinct pairs of chromosomes in their somatic cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). A total of 78.34 billion high-quality bases (236-fold genome coverage) were assembled into a 330.79-Mb mulberry genome with a scaffold N50 length of 390,115âbp and contig N50 length of 34,476âbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Tables S1 and S2</a>). There were 16,281âkb (4.9%) gaps and 314,510âkb (95.1%) non-gapped continuous sequences in the final assembly. We selected 10.46âGb high-quality sequenced short reads from the library with an average insert size of 500âbp to calculate the distribution of K-mer depth, defined as 17âbp here. A total of 8,577,674,309 17-mer were obtained and the genome size of <i>M. notabilis</i> was determined to be 357.4âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Methods</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S3</a>). Over 80% of the assembly was represented by 681 scaffolds and the largest scaffold was 3,477,367âbp, with 93.96% of bases covered by more than 20 reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S2</a>) and 97% of 10,000 random expressed sequence tags (ESTs) more than 90% covered by a scaffold (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S4</a>). The 35.02% GC content of the mulberry genome is similar to that of other eudicots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Cytological analysis of M. notabilis chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Cytological analysis of <i>M. notabilis</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="907" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Cytological detection of <i>M. notabilis</i> chromosomes. (<b>b</b>) Chromosome karyotyping of <i>M. notabilis</i>. Scale bar, 10âÎ¼m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics of the <i>M. notabilis</i> genome sequencing and assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms3445/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequences</h3><p>A combination of both <i>de novo</i> repeat prediction and homology-based search against the Repbase library (v15.02) resulted in 127.98âMb repetitive sequences in the non-gapped mulberry genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S5</a>). The transposable element (TE) content in the mulberry genome was probably underestimated because of the inherent limitations of <i>de novo</i> sequencing in dealing with repetitive sequences. After the exclusion of âNâs, according to the average coverage depth and the total reads mapped to the repetitive-sequence (~127.7âMB) and non-repetitive-sequence regions (~166.0âMb) in the mulberry genome, we estimated that there are about 18.48âMb repetitive sequences in the unassembled sequences. Hence, up to ~47% of the mulberry genome is composed of repetitive sequences. The proportion of repetitive sequences in the mulberry genome is comparable with that in apple (42%), whereas it is slightly higher than that in poplar (35%). More than 50% of mulberry repetitive sequences could be clearly classified into known categories, such as <i>Gypsy</i>-like (6.58%) and <i>Copia</i>-like (6.84%) long-terminal repeat retrotransposons. About 99.11% of TEs had a &gt;10% divergence rate, indicating that most mulberry TEs are relatively ancient (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>We identified 27,085 high-confidence protein-coding loci with complete gene structures in the mulberry genome, using 21âGb RNA-seq data from five tissues and 5,833 unique ESTs for gene model prediction and validation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Method</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S6</a>). Of the 27,085 predicted genes, 99.93% were supported by <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, 58.38% (15,811 genes) by RNA-seq/EST and 69.94% (18,943 genes) by homology-based approaches. More than half (52.19%) of the genes were supported by all three methods. Including 2,253 partial genes annotated by RNA-seq data and ESTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S7</a>), we predicted 29,338 genes with an average mRNA length of 2,849âbp, an average coding gene length of 1,156âbp and a mean number of 4.6 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S8</a>). Of these genes, 60.8% were supported by RNA-seq data and 76.92% (22,566/29,338) had homologous targets in functional databases, such as the NCBI non-redundant protein, Swissprot, InterPro, KEGG (Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes) and COG (Clusters of Orthologous Groups; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S9</a>).</p><p>On the basis of the RNA-seq data, we calculated the tissue specificity index <i>Ï</i>, to screen for tissue-specific genes and housekeeping genes. We found that 241, 213, 285, 360 and 404 genes specifically expressed in the root, bark, winter bud, male flower and leaf, respectively. In comparison, 1,805 genes were expressed constitutively in the 5 tissue/organs, including 116 encoding ribosomal proteins and 26 encoding translation initiation factors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome evolution</h3><p>Comparison of the mulberry genome to a rich collection of Rosales genome sequences, including <i>Cannabis sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="van Bakel, H. et al. The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 12, R102 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1641">9</a></sup>, <i>Malus domestica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833â839 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1647">10</a></sup> and <i>Fragaria vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1653">11</a></sup>, offers insights into levels and patterns of DNA-level diversity in this important clade. A phylogenetic tree based on single-copy mulberry genes and other 12 sequenced plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>) supports Moraceae as one of the closest relatives of Rosaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Morgan, D. R., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Robertson, K. R. Systematic and evolutionary implications of rbcL sequence variation in Rosaceae. Am. J. Bot. 81, 890â903 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1661">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 161, 105â121 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1664">13</a></sup>. The results suggest the speciation times of 63.5 million years ago (mya) for mulberry and <i>C. sativa</i> (Cannabaceae), 88.2âmya for mulberry and apple/strawberry (Rosaceae), and 101.6âmya for mulberry and <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (Fabales)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Crepet, W. L., Nixon, K. C. &amp; Gandolfo, M. A. Fossil evidence and phylogeny: the age of major angiosperm clades based on mesofossil and macrofossil evidence from Cretaceous deposits. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1666â1682 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1674">14</a></sup>. <i>Ks</i> plots suggest that mulberry (Moraceae) and <i>C. sativa</i> diverged later than the divergence of apple and strawberry in the Rosaceae family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic relationships of 13 plant species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Phylogenetic relationships of 13 plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="453" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The species are: <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>P. bretschneideri</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. persica</i>, <i>F. vesca</i>, <i>C. sativa</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>. The scale bar indicates 7.5 million years. The values at the branch points indicated the estimates of divergence time (mya) with a 95% credibility interval.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Ks distribution plot."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: <i>Ks</i> distribution plot.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link"
