aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="698" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The red, magenta, green and yellow lines represent <i>Ks</i> distribution of orthologous gene pairs in <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>C. sativa</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>F. vesca, M. notabilis</i>â<i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>M. truncatula</i>, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Different gene groups of several plants were then used to construct three phylogenetic trees (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). First, we used single-copy genes in the predicted mulberry gene data sets and their best-matched ones in other species to reconstruct phylogeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Second, we used single-copy genes of genewise-predicted mulberry genes to reconstruct phylogeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Third, we used best-matched genes in collinear positions across different genomes to reconstruct phylogeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). In all of the reconstructed phylogenetic trees, the branch of mulberry is longer than those of the other species, suggesting that mulberry evolved much (~3 times) faster than other Rosales.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenetic trees of M. notabilis and other plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Phylogenetic trees of <i>M. notabilis</i> and other plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="754" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Different data set were used to construct a phylogeny of the considered species. (<b>a</b>) A tree constructed using 136 single genes in the predicted <i>M. notabilis</i> gene data sets and their best-matched ones. (<b>b</b>) A tree constructed using 62 single genes predicted by Genewise in 10 plants. (<b>c</b>) A tree constructed using 318 best-matched collinear genes across 6 plant genomes. The scale of a unit is shown below each tree and the number on it shows how many amino acid substitutions per sites.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To investigate the syntenic and evolutionary relationship of the mulberry genome, without any available genetic map, <i>in-silico</i> gene staining or genome zipper approach was performed against the strawberry (<i>F. vesca</i>) genome sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mayer, K. F. et al. Gene content and virtual gene order of barley chromosome 1H. Plant Physiol. 151, 496â505 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1857">15</a></sup>. The gene density distribution of the conserved syntenic regions against strawberry was computed and visualized as a heatmap using a sliding window approach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig5">Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="In-silico staining of M. notabilis gene models against F. vesca."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: <i>In-silico</i> staining of <i>M. notabilis</i> gene models against <i>F. vesca</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="366" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Using a sliding window approach (500âkb), the total gene density (upper track) and the relative distribution of orthologous genes (lower track) were calculated for <i>M. notabilis</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Alignment of mulberry scaffolds to their best-matched grape chromosomal regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a>) often revealed two additional but less pronounced homologous regions, indicating that mulberry shares the eudicot-common triplication revealed initially by the analysis of the grape genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1906">16</a></sup>. Similarly, a region of the strawberry or cannabis genomes often has one primary and two secondary homologous grape genome regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig6">Fig. 6b,c</a>), contrary to an earlier report of no paleopolypoidization in strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109â116 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d20400e1913">11</a></sup>. The fact that mulberry, strawberry and cannabis have the pan-eudicot hexaploidization as their most recent polyploidy is further supported by the distribution of synonymous nucleotide substitution rates of homologous genes in collinear blocks within and between these genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#Fig6">Fig. 6d,e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Dotplots of species and Ks distributions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Dotplots of species and <i>Ks</i> distributions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3445_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="735" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>V. vinifera</i> (<b>a</b>), <i>F. vesca</i>â<i>V. vinifera</i> (<b>b</b>), <i>C. sativa</i>â<i>V. vinifera</i> (<b>c</b>) and <i>Ks</i> distribution of within-each-plant homologues (<b>d</b>) and between-different-plant homologues (<b>e</b>) in collinearity. For <i>M. notabilis</i> and <i>C. sativa</i>, gene coding DNA sequences of <i>V. vinifera</i> were searched against their genomes by using BLASTN, and their hit locations were found. This BLASTN information was used to produce the dotplots. Unanchored scaffolds were linked together as to their best-matched grape genomic regions, and the putative pseudochromosomal regions of <i>M. notabilis</i> and <i>C. sativa</i> genomes were produced. For <i>F. vesca</i>, proteinâprotein searches using BLASTP were conducted to reveal putative homologous genes, and this information was used to make dotplot; along chromosomes, genes were placed with their chromosomal order as coordinates.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3445/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Diversifying selection</h3><p>The divergent morphologies and phytochemistries for which various Rosales are cultivated may reflect diversifying selections on orthologous genes. By regression analysis between the <i>Ï</i>, the non-synonymous (<i>Ka</i>) versus synonymous (<i>Ks</i>) nucleotide substitution rate ratio (<i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i>) and the <i>Ks</i> values, we estimated that 307, 338, 353 and 197 gene pairs have significantly higher-than-average non-synonymous (<i>Ka</i>) versus synonymous (<i>Ks</i>) nucleotide substitution rate ratios (<i>Ï</i>), indicating diversifying selection for <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>C. sativa</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>F. vesca</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>M. domestica</i> and <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>M. truncatula</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Interestingly, for the subset of genes that meet the more stringent Fisherâs exact test, diversifying selection between 222 pairs of <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>C. sativa</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Fig. S6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S10</a>) is enriched in aging and stress response-related genes, perhaps linked to the difference in life expectancy of the plants. In <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>F. vesca</i> and <i>M. notabilis</i>â<i>M. domestica</i> comparisons, 228 and 258 diversifying selected orthologous pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 2</a>) may be associated with functional differences, for example, <i>Morus000754</i> (mulberry)â<i>MDP0000252168</i> (apple) and <i>Morus009486</i> (mulberry)â<i>MDP0000290357</i> (apple) involved in cutin biosynthetic processes may be related to the appleâs thick cuticle (although mechanisms of cuticle biogenesis are not clear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yeats, T. H. et al. Mining the surface proteome of tomato (Solanum lycopersicum) fruit for proteins associated with cuticle biogenesis. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 3759â3771 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2107">17</a></sup>). Particularly prominent in the mulberryâRosaceae (apple, strawberry) diversification are the gene pairs related to plastid components (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 3 and 4</a>), suggesting that Rubisco<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Kapralov, M. V. &amp; Filatov, D. A. Widespread positive selection in the photosynthetic Rubisco enzyme. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 73 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2114">18</a></sup> and many plastid genes were under positive diversifying selection.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Resistance genes</h3><p>The mulberry genome has 142 nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-containing resistance (R) genes that constitute about 0.53% of all <i>Morus</i> genes, comparable to that of <i>Arabidopsis</i> (0.52%) and strawberry (0.58%), and lower than that of poplar (0.86%) and apple (1.49%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S11</a>). All of these R genes were classified into six groups, TIR-NBS-LRR, CC-NBS-LRR, NBS-LRR, NBS, CC-NBS and TIR-NBS, with the maximum number of 46 belonging to the CC-NBS-LRR group. The <i>Morus</i> genome contains 127 cysteine protease (CP; 0.47%) and 129 aspartic protease (AP; 0.48%) encoding genes, which is comparable to that of apple (0.59%, 0.37%) and of strawberry (0.49%, 0.53%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 6 and 7</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S12</a>). Prominent among these are 13 CP and 4 AP genes expressed in the laticifers of mulberry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S13</a>). Interestingly, one of the four AP genes (<i>Morus008067</i>) is under diversifying selection with an apple gene (<i>MDP0000201076</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Data 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Protease inhibitor genes</h3><p>To alleviate insect infestation, plants have evolved a defence mechanism to interfere with the digestive systems of insects by expressing a number of plant protease inhibitors (PIs). On the basis of the known PI sequences and their conserved domains, we identified 79 PIs in the mulberry genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S14</a>). Twenty-two family C1 cysteine peptidase inhibitor genes and 19 family A1/C1 serine peptidase inhibitor genes were annotated in the <i>Morus</i> genome, accounting for half of the identified inhibitor genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Mulberry miRNAs identified in silkworm tissues</h3><p>Adaptation of silkworm to the seasonal growth of mulberry leaves may involve cross-kingdom molecular signalling. By aligning the <i>Morus</i> genome to various plant small RNA databases, we predicted 311 small nuclear RNAs and 223 miRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S15</a>). Five of the mulberry miRNAs, absent in the silkworm genome, were found in the miRNA database derived from silkworm larval haemolymph (two), anterior-middle silk glands (two), and posterior silk glands (one) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3445#MOESM244">Supplementary Table S16</a>). The sequencing of small RNAs was repeated using a different batch of silkworm haemolymph. The presence of the mulberry miRNAs in silkworm haemolymph identified in an earlier database was confirmed in the repeat experiment.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Early studies proposed a basic chromosome number of 14 for mulberry species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Janaki Ammal, E. The origin of black mulberry. J. R. Hortic. Soc. 73, 117â120 (1948)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2200">19</a></sup>. This number is widely cited in the literature even though later cytological studies on two <i>M. indica</i> species proposed a basic chromosome number of 7 for <i>Morus</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Datta, M. Cytogenetical studies on two species of Morus. Cytologia (Tokyo) 19, 86â95 (1954)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2210">20</a></sup>. The diverse levels of polyploidization in the genus are reflected in the wide range of chromosome numbers: 14 in <i>M. notabilis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yu, M. D. et al. The discovery and study on a natural haploid Morus notabilis Schneid. Sci. Sericult. 22, 67â71 (1996) (Chinese writing)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2216">21</a></sup>, 28 in <i>M. indica</i> or <i>M. alba</i>, 42 in <i>M. bombycis</i> and even 308 in <i>M. nigra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tojyo, I. Studies on the polypolid in mulberry tree (IV) On the flower and pollen grains of one race in Morus nigra L. J. Sericult. Sci. Jpn 35, 360â364 (1966) (Japanese)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2232">22</a></sup>. Because of the high complexity of polyploid genomes, the species (<i>M. notabilis</i>) with 14 chromosomes is chosen for whole-genome sequencing. To verify the number of chromosomes of the <i>M. notabilis</i>, somatic cells at metaphase stage in the apical bud was used for cytological analyses. We confirmed that the cells of <i>M. notabilis</i> contained 14 chromosomes. Chromosome karyotyping clearly grouped the 14 chromosomes of <i>M. notabilis</i> into seven distinct pairs, supporting the basic chromosome number of seven proposed in the studies on <i>M. indica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Datta, M. Cytogenetical studies on two species of Morus. Cytologia (Tokyo) 19, 86â95 (1954)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2251">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis indicated that the mulberry genes form a clade with those of other Rosales. Moraceae, conventionally considered as belonging to Urticales, is thought to be one of the closest relatives of Rosaceae. However, a recent report suggested that the families Ulmaceae, Cannabaceae, Moraceae and Urticaceae belong to a single clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, S., Soltis, D. E., Yang, Y., Li, D. &amp; Yi, T. Multi-gene analysis provides a well-supported phylogeny of Rosales. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 60, 21â28 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2258">23</a></sup>, named as the urticalean rosids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sytsma, K. J. et al. Urticalean rosids: circumscription, rosid ancestry, and phylogenetics based on rbcL, trnL-F, and ndhF sequences. Am. J. Bot. 89, 1531â1546 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2262">24</a></sup>. Moraceae was later classified into Rosales by the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group III<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 161, 105â121 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2266">13</a></sup>. Our results support this reclassification.</p><p>Mulberry is rapidly evolving at the nucleotide level. Itâs fast evolving genes may have contributed to the flexibility of mulberry to adapt to environments outside of its native range, facilitating its spread to Europe, Africa and the United States. In contrast to its rapid nucleotide changes, Rosales ploidies have evolved conservatively. Mulberry, strawberry, cannabis, papaya and grape underwent the most recent pan-eudicot hexaploidization. Widespread neopolyploidy in mulberry with up to 308 (44 Ã ) chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tojyo, I. Studies on the polypolid in mulberry tree (IV) On the flower and pollen grains of one race in Morus nigra L. J. Sericult. Sci. Jpn 35, 360â364 (1966) (Japanese)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2273">22</a></sup> and strawberry with up to 70, suggest an intriguing scenario that these lineages may be receptive to the benefits of a new wave of polyploidization.</p><p>Mulberry is a woody perennial tree and constant pruning is a common practice not only to collect leaves for silkworms but also to boost leaf production. Pruning increases risk of pest infestation and pathogen infection; therefore, a robust defence system helps to fend off these biotic stresses. Proteins encoded by plant R genes allow the recognition of pathogen effectors, such as their cognate avirulence gene products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Mansfield, J. W. From bacterial avirulence genes to effector functions via the hrp delivery system: an overview of 25 years of progress in our understanding of plant innate immunity. Mol. Plant Pathol. 10, 721â734 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2280">25</a></sup>. Most of the extensively studied plant R genes are NBS-containing R genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Meyers, B. C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R. S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129â134 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2284">26</a></sup>. In the mulberry genome, we identified a total of 142 NBS-containing R genes. Mulberry is a lactiferous plant and protein components, such as the chitinase-like protein, in mulberry latexes are believed to be involved in the defence system against microbes or herbivores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wasano, N. et al. A unique latex protein, MLX56, defends mulberry trees from insects. Phytochemistry 70, 880â888 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2288">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kitajima, S. et al. Two chitinase-like proteins abundantly accumulated in latex of mulberry show insecticidal activity. BMC Biochem. 11, 6 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2291">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kitajima, S. et al. Comparative study of gene expression and major proteinsâ function of laticifers in lignified and unlignified organs of mulberry. Planta 235, 589â601 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2294">29</a></sup>. Cysteine proteases in the laticifers of papaya and aspartic proteases secreted into the pitcher of <i>Nepenthes alata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Konno, K. et al. Papain protects papaya trees from herbivorous insects: role of cysteine proteases in latex. Plant J. 37, 370â378 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2300">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="An, C. I., Fukusaki, E. &amp; Kobayashi, A. Aspartic proteinases are expressed in pitchers of the carnivorous plant Nepenthes alata Blanco. Planta 214, 661â667 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2303">31</a></sup> have also been shown to be toxic to herbivorous insects. Sequencing of mulberry genome revealed 127 CP genes and 129 aspartic protease genes. The functional studies of these genes will expand our knowledge on mulberry defence mechanisms.</p><p>It remains unclear how the oligophagous silkworm bypasses plant defence mechanisms that interfere with insect digestive systems. In particular, plant PIs reduce the activity of the digestive enzymes in the guts of herbivorous insects, resulting in serious developmental malformations, lethality and reduced procreation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="BayÃ©s, A. et al. Structural basis of the resistance of an insect carboxypeptidase to plant protease inhibitors. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 16602 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2311">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Li, C., Song, X., Li, G. &amp; Wang, P. Midgut cysteine protease-inhibiting activity in Trichoplusia ni protects the peritrophic membrane from degradation by plant cysteine proteases. Insect Biochem. Mol. Biol. 39, 726â734 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2314">33</a></sup>. Previous studies reported that plants produce more PIs with multidomains and multimeric structures, which have antinutritional effects on <i>Spodoptera frugiperda</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Beekwilder, J. &amp; Jongsma, M. Co-evolution of insect proteases and plant protease inhibitors. Curr. Protein Pept. Sci. 12, 437â447 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2320">34</a></sup>. The insect circumvents plant PIs via inducible PI-insensitive proteases and the degradation of plant PIs by specific proteases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ferreira, C., Capella, A. N., Sitnik, R. &amp; Terra, W. R. Properties of the digestive enzymes and the permeability of the peritrophic membrane of Spodoptera frugiperda (Lepidoptera) larvae. Comp. Biochem. Phys. A 107, 631â640 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2324">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zavala, J. A., Giri, A. P., Jongsma, M. A. &amp; Baldwin, I. T. Digestive duet: midgut digestive proteinases of Manduca sexta ingesting Nicotiana attenuata with manipulated trypsin proteinase inhibitor expression. PloS One 3, e2008 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2327">36</a></sup>. The diamondback moth, <i>Plutella xylostella</i>, a notorious Lepidopteran pest of cruciferous crops, inactivates mustard trypsin inhibitor 2 to break through host plant defence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Yang, L., Fang, Z., Dicke, M., Loon, J. J. A. &amp; Jongsma, M. A. The diamondback moth, Plutella xylostella, specifically inactivates Mustard Trypsin Inhibitor 2 (MTI2) to overcome host plant defence. Insect Biochem. Mol. Biol. 39, 55â61 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2334">37</a></sup>. Parallel transcriptome analysis of the silkwormâmulberry oligophagy, benefiting from the respective genome sequences may accelerate our understanding of the fundamentals in plantâherbivore adaptation.</p><p>A total of five mulberry miRNAs were found in the silkworm sequencing data. None of them seem to come from the silkworm genome. One of them, MIR156, is abundantly expressed in the old leaves at the vegetative growth stage of rice and has a major role in the juvenile-to-adult transition in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Xie, K. et al. Gradual increase of miR156 regulates temporal expression changes of numerous genes during leaf development in rice. Plant Physiol. 158, 1382â1394 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2341">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wang, J. W., Czech, B. &amp; Weigel, D. miR156-regulated SPL transcription factors define an endogenous flowering pathway in Arabidopsis thaliana. Cell 138, 738â749 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2344">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wang, J. W. et al. miRNA control of vegetative phase change in trees. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002012 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2347">40</a></sup>. Noting that rice MIR168a can be transferred to human and regulate the low-density lipoprotein receptor adaptor protein 1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhang, L. et al. Exogenous plant MIR168a specifically targets mammalian LDLRAP1: evidence of cross-kingdom regulation by microRNA. Cell Res. 22, 107â126 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2350">41</a>), it remains unclear whether mulberry MIR156 in silkgland signals leaf aging and stimulates cocoon spinning, or whether tissue-specific presence of other mulberry MIRs has a role in coordinating development of silkworm.</p><p>In summary, genomic information is an important resource for modern genetic research of mulberry. The genomic features of mulberry, such as gene families, segmental duplication, and syntenic blocks not only enrich the data available for plant comparative genomics but also accelerate future identification of target genes from closely related species of the family Moraceae. Genetic markers can be developed based on these genome sequences for studies involving genetic map construction, positional cloning, strain identification and marker-assisted selection. These molecular tools and genomic techniques will accelerate agricultural improvement. As a model system for studies of plantâherbivore relationships, the availability of the mulberry and silkworm genome sequences offers a unique opportunity to gain insights into such biological partnerships prevalent in most terrestrial habitats.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Karyotype analysis of <i>M. notabilis</i> C.K. Schn</h3><p>Young leaves were treated with 2âmM 8-hydroxy-quinoline for 3âh at room temperature, and then fixed in 3:1 methanol/glacial acetic acid for 2âh at 4âÂ°C. Fixed leaves were incubated with 1/15âM KCl solution for 30âmin and digested by 2.5% (W/V) cellulose (YaKult Co., Japan) and 2.5% (W/V) pectolyase (YaKult Co.) for 1.5âh at 37âÂ°C. Digested leaves were treated with ddH<sub>2</sub>O for 10âmin and post-fixed in 3:1 methanol/glacial acetic acid for 30âmin at room temperature. Post-fixed leaves were smashed and two drops of cell suspension were added on a glass slide for Giemsa staining at room temperature for 6âh. Slides were analysed under a microscope (Olympus Corporation, Japan).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">DNA and RNA preparation</h3><p>A wild mulberry species, <i>M. notabilis</i>, with a chromosome number of 14 was used for genome sequencing. Genomic DNA used as a template for the library construction was extracted from the winter buds by a CTAB method. Total RNA was isolated from five tissues (root; 1-year-old branch bark; winter bud; male flower; leaf) according to the methods of Wan and Wilkins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wan, C. Y. &amp; Wilkins, T. A. A modified hot borate method significantly enhances the yield of high-quality RNA from cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.). Anal. Biochem. 223, 7â12 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2386">42</a></sup>, and was treated with RNase-free DNase I for 30âmin at 37âÂ°C (New England BioLabs) to remove residual DNA. Beads with oligo(dT) were used to isolate poly(A) mRNA. First-strand complementary DNA was synthesized using random hexamer primers and reverse transcriptase (Invitrogen). The second-strand cDNA was synthesized using DNA polymerase I (New England BioLabs) in the presence of RNase H (Invitrogen).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing</h3><p>A whole-genome shotgun approach was used to sequence the mulberry genome. Sequencing libraries were prepared following the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina, San Diego, CA). For short-insert DNA libraries, 5âÎ¼g of genomic DNA was fragmented by nebulization with compressed nitrogen gas. The DNA ends were blunted with an âAâ base to the ends of the DNA fragments. Next, the DNA adaptors (Illumina) with a single âTâ base overhang at the 3â²-end were ligated to the DNA fragment. We then purified the ligation products on a 2% agarose gel, and excised and purified gel slices for each insert size (Qiagen Gel Extraction Kit). For long (â¥2âKb), mate-paired libraries, 10â30âÎ¼g genomic DNA was fragmented by nebulization with compressed nitrogen gas. We then used biotin-labelled dNTPs for polishing and gel selection for the main bands of 2, 5 and 10âKb. The DNA fragments were then circularized for self-ligation. The two ends of the DNA fragment were merged together and the linear DNA fragments were digested by DNA exonuclease. The circularized DNA was fragmented again, followed by enrichment of the âmerged endsâ with magnetic beads using biotin and streptavidin interaction, then the ends were blunted, and âAâ base and adaptors were added. We followed the manufacturerâs instructions (Illumina) for paired-end (PE) sequencing runs by the following workflow: cluster generation, template hybridization, isothermal amplification, linearization, blocking, denaturing and hybridization of sequencing primers. The base-calling pipeline (SolexaPipeline-0.3) was used to obtain sequences from the raw fluorescent images.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly</h3><p>Before <i>de novo</i> assembly, we filtered the low-quality data by the following five steps: (1) trim the low-quality bases on both 5â²- and 3â²-end of each read according to quality reports from Hiseq2000 pipeline; (2) discard those reads with Ns&gt;10% of the read length; (3) remove those reads when the total low-quality bases (<i>Q</i>&lt;8) was &gt;50% of the read length; (4) discard the reads contaminated by adapters; and (5) remove duplicated reads caused by PCR during library construction. SOAPdenovo is a genome assembler developed in BGI-Shenzhen and this software preformed <i>de Bruijn</i> graph algorithm assemblies in a stepwise strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li, R., Li, Y., Kristiansen, K. &amp; Wang, J. SOAP: short oligonucleotide alignment program. Bioinformatics 24, 713â714 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2415">43</a></sup>. We first assembled short reads from fragmented small insert-size (&lt;1âkb) libraries into contigs using 49-kmers. We then realigned all the reads to contig sequences with 41-kmers and compiled all aligned reads to the available contigs. According to the PE information, we joined the contigs into scaffolds by seven steps from 170âbp insert-size libraries to 20âkb insert-size libraries. To fill the gaps in scaffolds, we collected the PE reads, one of which uniquely aligned to a contig and the other located in gaps, to repeat a local assembly. The intra-scaffold gaps were filled by local assembly using the reads from a read pair with one end uniquely aligned to a contig and the other in a gap.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">TEs and repetitive DNA</h3><p>To predict the TEs in the mulberry genome, we first constructed a TE library with RepeatModeler (version 1.0.3, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>), RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005) &#xA;                    http://bix.ucsd.edu/repeatscout/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2435">44</a></sup> (version 1.0.5, <a href="http://bix.ucsd.edu/repeatscout/">http://bix.ucsd.edu/repeatscout/</a>) and Piler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152âi158 (2005) &#xA;                    http://www.drive5.com/piler/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2446">45</a></sup> (version 1.0, <a href="http://www.drive5.com/piler/">http://www.drive5.com/piler/</a>), and then performed <i>de novo</i> prediction of TEs on it using RepeatMasker (version 3.2.9, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0. URL &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2468">46</a></sup>. RepeatMasker and ProteinMask (version 3.0) were also used to find known TEs with a TE library composed of Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005) &#xA;                    http://www.girinst.org/repbase/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2472">47</a></sup> (version 15.02, <a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase/">http://www.girinst.org/repbase/</a>) and eudicot TEs from TIGR (version 3.0, <a href="http://plantta.jcvi.org">http://plantta.jcvi.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Pertea, G. et al. TIGR Gene Indices clustering tools (TGICL): a software system for fast clustering of large EST datasets. Bioinformatics 19, 651â652 (2003) &#xA;                    http://plantta.jcvi.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2491">48</a></sup>. Tandem Repeats Finder (version 4.04, <a href="http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>) was used to identify tandem repeats. Simple repeats, satellite sequences and low-complexity repeats were identified by RepeatMasker with the option of â-nointâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2502">49</a></sup>. The classified TE families in the <i>M. notabilis</i> genome were aligned to the consensus sequences in the Repbase library (v15.02) and the sequence divergence rates of TEs were determined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Three methods were used to predict the mulberry genes: a homology-based method, a <i>de novo</i> method and an EST/transcript-based method. High-confidence set of genes were predicted by both homology-based and <i>de novo</i> methods. For the annotation of the mulberry protein-coding genes, we searched the nucleotide sequences of 27,085 high-confidence genes against NCBI, KEGG, COG and Swissport databases with a minimal <i>e</i>-value of 1eâ5. Protein domains and functions of predicted 27,085 amino acid sequences of mulberry were annotated with Iprscan (v4.4.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">RNA-seq and EST sequencing</h3><p>The cDNA libraries were prepared and sequenced according to Illuminaâs protocols. TopHat (v1.3.3) was used to align these RNA-seq reads to the mulberry genome. The reads per kb per million reads values were calculated to measure the gene expression levels of the five tissues, and the tissue specificity index <i>Ï</i> was computed to identify the specific expressed genes in each tissue. For EST sequencing, RNA samples from the same five tissues were combined for cDNA synthesis using Creator SMART cDNA Kit (Clontech). A normalized cDNA library was constructed with Trimmer-Director kit (Evrogen). Ten thousand randomly chosen clones from the normalized library were sequenced using ABI3730 (Applied Biosystem).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Non-coding RNA genes</h3><p>The transfer RNAs in the <i>M. notabilis</i> genome were found using tRNAscan-SE (v1.23) with the âeukaryotesâ option<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 0955 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2548">50</a></sup>. The <i>M. notabilis</i> genome was aligned to plant ribosomal RNAs with BLASTN (<i>e</i>-value, 1eâ5), and rRNAs with sequence identity &gt;85% and heat shock protein length longer than 50âbp were recorded. The <i>M. notabilis</i> genome was aligned to the Rfam database (v 9.1) with BLASTN (<i>e</i>-value, 1). The raw output was further analysed by the INFERNAL software, which was used to predict miRNA and small nuclear RNA by searching DNA sequence databases owing to the RNA structure and sequence similarities.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21"><i>In-silico</i> gene staining</h3><p>We used BLASTP (<i>e</i>-value, 1eâ5) to identify reciprocal best-hit orthologous gene pairs between mulberry and strawberry. This reciprocal best-hit matrix and the orthologous gene pairs were used to further define the syntenic blocks between two species in the MCscan pipeline. The scaffolds of mulberry with syntenic blocks were aligned together according to the syntenic order in the strawberry linkage groups using Genome Zipper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mayer, K. F. et al. Gene content and virtual gene order of barley chromosome 1H. Plant Physiol. 151, 496â505 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2579">15</a></sup>. The distributions of gene density and orthologous gene density were calculated using a 500-kb sliding window approach.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Identification of mulberry miRNAs in silkworm tissues</h3><p>The small RNA was extracted from 12âml of silkworm haemolymph (collected from the fifth instar day-5 larvae) using mirVana PARIS kit (Ambion, USA). The sequencing of small RNA in haemolymph was conducted following the procedure describe by Liu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Liu, S. et al. MicroRNAs of Bombyx mori identified by Solexa sequencing. BMC Genomics 11, 148 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2591">51</a></sup>. The sequences of small RNA in the anterior-middle and posterior silk glands were downloaded from <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gds?term=GSE17965">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gds?term=GSE17965</a>. The small RNA sequenced data of three silkworm tissues were used as queries to search against mulberry-predicted miRNAs by BLASTN without mismatch. The sequences aligned to silkworm genome, rRNAs and tRNAs were filtered out.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Phylogenetic tree and determine the speciation time</h3><p>Single-copy genes from 13 plant species were used to reconstruct a phylogenetic tree based on the maximum likelihood method. Orthologous gene pairs had been determined by top-ranked BLAST hits in each other with an <i>e</i>-value 1eâ10. The <i>Ks</i> value<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418â426 (1986)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2617">52</a></sup> between the orthologous pairs were calculated by the yn00 programme in PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2621">53</a></sup>. The speciation time base on <i>Ks</i> value was dated by the equation <i>T</i>=<i>Ks</i>/2<i>Î»</i> with <i>Î»</i>=6.1 Ã 10<sup>â9</sup> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151â1155 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2642">54</a>). Orthologous gene pairs likely to be under positive (diversifying) selection between mulberry and each of the other four plants were determined by regression analysis between <i>Ka</i> and <i>Ks</i> values based on a 95% prediction interval range<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Sato, S. et al. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2653">55</a></sup>. Gene pairs with <i>Ï</i>-values greater than the prediction interval upper limit were considered to show evidence of positive selection. Gene Ontology groups in which the high omega pairs were significantly included were determined by BLAST2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="GÃ¶tz, S. et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, 3420â3435 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2660">56</a></sup> with a cut-off <i>P</i>-value&lt;0.05 using Fisherâs exact test.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Inference of gene collinearity</h3><p>We inferred gene collinearity with MCSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2675">57</a></sup>, a multiple-chromosome alignment tool, complemented by analyses using COLINEARSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang, X. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 447 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2679">58</a></sup>, a pairwise-chromosome alignment tool. The inferred collinear genes were used to perform phylogenetic and evolutionary analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Dating evolutionary event</h3><p>We used collinear genes between plants, and within-each-plant homologues with high confidence, to infer evolutionary events. For example, collinear genes between mulberry scaffolds are likely to have resulted from ancient polyploidization event(s) if present; and collinear genes between mulberry and grape are likely to have resulted from a divergence of the two species. The synonymous nucleotide substitution rates (<i>Ks</i>) were calculated by using NeiâGojobori approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418â426 (1986)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2694">52</a></sup> implemented in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2698">53</a></sup>. The distributions of <i>Ks</i> values were drawn to infer the relative time of evolutionary events.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Homologous dotplotting</h3><p>We used predicted gene sets that are described above and a gene data set predicted by Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms3445#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d20400e2713">59</a></sup> in the analysis. Genome sequences and annotations of grape, apple, strawberry and cannabis were downloaded from online databases, and the most up-to-date versions till October 2012 were used in the analyses. In comparison with genomes with available pseudochromosomes, we used proteinâprotein searches using BLASTP to reveal putative homologous genes, and the output was used to make dotplot; genes were placed along with their chromosomal order as coordinates. When a comparison was done involving genomes (for example, cannabis and mulberry) without available pseudochromosomes, that is, those with unanchored scaffolds, gene coding DNA sequences from a genome sequences with pseudochromosomes (for example, grape) were searched against the cannabis and mulberry genomes using BLASTN, and hits on the pseudochromosomes were located. The BLASTN output was used to produce dotplots. To detect the genome duplication events, the unanchored scaffolds were linked to their best-matched grape genomic regions on the putative pseudochromosomes. The putative pseudochromosomal regions of mulberry and cannabis were identified this way. A corresponding grape region would have two matched regions clustered together in the dotplot.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Data used in this study</h3><p>The genome data were downloaded from the following websites and are associated with the accession codes provided.</p><p><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR9), <a href="ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/Genes/TAIR9_genome_release/">ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/Genes/TAIR9_genome_release/</a>, GCA_000001735.1.</p><p><i>C. sativa</i>, <a href="http://genome.ccbr.utoronto.ca/downloads.html">http://genome.ccbr.utoronto.ca/downloads.html</a>, GCA_000230575.1.</p><p><i>Carica papaya</i> (version 1th), <a href="http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn/page/cucumber/download.jsp">http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn/page/cucumber/download.jsp</a>, GCA_000004075.1.</p><p><i>F. vesca</i> (version 1.1), <a href="http://www.rosaceae.org/species/fragaria/fragaria_vesca/genome_v1.1">http://www.rosaceae.org/species/fragaria/fragaria_vesca/genome_v1.1</a>, GCA_000184155.1.</p><p><i>Glycine max</i> (version 1.0), <a href="ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v5.0/Gmax/">ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v5.0/Gmax/</a>, GCA_000004515.1.</p><p><i>M. domestica</i> (version 1.0), <a href="http://genomics.research.iasma.it/index.html">http://genomics.research.iasma.it/index.html</a>, GCA_000148765.2.</p><p><i>M. truncatula</i>, <a href="ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v8.0/Mtruncatula/">ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v8.0/Mtruncatula/</a>, GCA_000219495.1.</p><p><i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (version 5.0), <a href="ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v5.0/Ptrichocarpa/">ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v5.0/Ptrichocarpa/</a>, GCA_000002775.1.</p><p><i>Prunus persica</i>, <a href="ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v8.0/Ppersica/">ftp://ftp.jgi-psf.org/pub/compgen/phytozome/v8.0/Ppersica/</a>, GCA_000346465.1.</p><p><i>Pyrus bretschneideri</i>, <a href="http://peargenome.njau.edu.cn:8004/default.asp?d=1&amp;m=1">http://peargenome.njau.edu.cn:8004/default.asp?d=1&amp;m=1</a>, GCA_000315295.1.</p><p><i>Theobroma cacao</i> (version 1.0), <a href="http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/gbrowse/download.html">http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/gbrowse/download.html</a>, GCA_000403535.1.</p><p><i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/Download/Projets/Projet_ML/data/12X/">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/externe/Download/Projets/Projet_ML/data/12X/</a>, GCA_000003745.2.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec28" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec28-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec28">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec28-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> The <i>Morus</i> genome data has been deposited in the Genbank short-read archive (Bioproject: PRJNA202089; short reads: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA075563">SRA075563</a>). The version described in this paper is ATGF01000000. The miRNA data has been deposited in the Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) under the accession code <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE48168">GSE48168</a>.</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> He, N. <i>et al.</i> Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree <i>Morus notabilis</i>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 4:2445 doi: 10.1038/ncomms3445 (2013).</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM248"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_BFncomms3445_MOESM248_ESM.xls" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 5</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>NBS-containing resistance genes in the <i>M. notabilis</i> genome. (XLS 28 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM249"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_BFncomms3445_MOESM249_ESM.xlsx" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 6</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Predicted cysteine proteases in <i>M. notabilis</i> genome. (XLSX 21 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM250"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_BFncomms3445_MOESM250_ESM.xlsx" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Data 7</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Predicted aspartic proteases in <i>M. notabilis</i> genome. (XLSX 20 kb)</p></div></div>
                  
                <div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM251"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3445/MediaObjects/41467_2013_BFncomms3445_MOESM251_ESM.pdf" data-supp-info-image="">Supplementary Figures, Tables and Methods</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Supplementary Figures S1-S6, Supplementary Tables S1-S16 and Supplementary Methods (PDF 615 kb)</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="rightslink" data-title="Rights and permissions"><div class="c-article-section" id="rightslink-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="rightslink">Rights and permissions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="rightslink-content">
